\subsubsection{Background}\label{background}

The City of Dallas has a strong commmitment to transparency and
accountability. Citizens or interested parties are provided an
invaluable resource to easily access data published by the City. This
data acts as a bridge between the citizens of Dallas and the city. It
breakdowns barriers for information access by providing access to
information where one can instantly review, compare, visualize, analyze
and share. It is also a valuable resource for anyone wanting to move to
the Dallas area or start a business with instant access to crime rates,
hate crimes etc.

It allows the citizenry of Dallas to better understand how their city is
performing. It also allows citizens to be better informed and to more
effectively collaborate with policymakers and service providers. This
helps make Dallas a more open, accessible, transparent and collaborative
community.

The dataset is a valuable resource for citizens, business leaders and
civic leaders seeking to improve governance, public services and the
quality of life in Dallas.

It represents the Dallas Police Public Data - RMS Incidents from June 1,
2014 to September 7 2018. The information contained in this dataset
allows Dallasites to access accurate information in a timely manner.
Crime classifications are based on information supplied to the Dallas
Police Department. This information is preliminary and could be
reclassified later based on further investigation.

Crime classifications are based upon preliminary information supplied to
the Dallas Police Department by the reporting parties and the
preliminary classifications may be changed at a later date based upon
additional investigation. The data set suppresses sexually oriented
offenses, offenses where juveniles or children are the victim or
suspect, property items that are considered evidence, Social Service
Referral offenses, and identifying vehicle information in certain
offenses for the sake of security.

To ensure that privacy concerns are protected, and legal standards are
met, report data is "filtered" prior to being made available to the
public. Among the exclusions are:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Sexually oriented offenses
\item
  Offenses where juveniles or children (individuals under 17 years of
  age) are the victim or suspect
\item
  Listing of property items that are considered evidence
\item
  Social Service Referral offenses
\item
  Identifying vehicle information in certain offenses
\item
  From June 1, 2014 to September 7, 2018
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Business Objectives}\label{business-objectives}

Our analysis focused on classifying data into meaningful groups based on
type of crimes, victims, arrests and associated response times.
Furthermore, we wanted to examine the impact of population demographics
by stratifying crimes based on age, sex and ethnicity. Another question
of interest was the seasonality of crime cycles in the Dallas area and
we conducted a time series analysis to better understand the
seasonality.

We further used the Call received time, Call cleared time and Call
dispatch time to compute the responding officer's response time. We
further proceeded to identify a relationship between the response time
and the incident location, incident type, incident time (morning,
evening or night), witnesess involved or not etc.
Person\_Involvement\_Type can be used to analysis if the response time
fast if there is the witness involved int.

Another area of interest was to conduct a Time series analysis to
identify crime patterns over time. We elected to analyze the data set to
help us track response times for reported incidents in the Dallas region
using different grouping factors.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Incidents by Division
\item
  Victim Profile Analysis by Race, Age, City of incident, Offense type
\item
  Correlation between Arrests made and Response time
\item
  Conversion rate of dispatches to arrests by priority of calls
\item
  Response times relative to Location and Population density
\item
  Crime incidents as a percentage of the population of that area to
  understand crime rates
\item
  Median dispatch times by division
\item
  Track crime, Classification time, Response time
\item
  Crime incidents by days of week, months and across years
\item
  Cross validation, Dividing the data set into test and train
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Success Criteria}\label{success-criteria}

We should be able to accurately categorize and classify incidents,
Response times and crime patterns and be able to predict likelihood of
arrests. This will enable the police departments to better plan their
resourcing and training needs as well as provide superior service levels
to the community.

    \textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

\section{DATA MEANING TYPE}\label{data-meaning-type}

\textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

    The dataset contains \textbf{469,927} records with \textbf{100}
variables. The full schema of this dataset can be accessed here.

Our initial observations:

\textbf{Data Types}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  String or Text types: 70
\item
  Numeric fields: 19 (float64: 15 integer64: 4)
\item
  Date/Time fields: 9
\item
  Location field with X-Y coordinates: 1
\item
  Boolean fields Yes/No: 1
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Missing Values}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Attributes with no missing values: 45
\item
  Attributes with 1\% to 25\% missing values : 27
\item
  Attributes with 26\% to 50\% missing values : 7
\item
  Attributes with 51\% to 75\% missing values : 10
\item
  Attributes with 76\% to 90\% : 3
\item
  Attributes with greater than 90\% missing values: 8
\end{enumerate}

We proceeded with our analysis selecting attributes as per our initial
analysis above and researching to better understand what each attribute
represents in the dataset. We even had a discussion with an officer from
law enforcement to ensure we accurately represented what the attributes
meant.

    Below are the definitions and descriptions of attributes we selected for
our analysis:

    \end{Verbatim}

    \textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}
\# DATA QUALITY
\textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

    \subsubsection{Attribute Selection}\label{attribute-selection}

From the original dataset above, we selected 35 variables for our
analysis and eliminated 65 attributes in our analysis for the following
reasons:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Redundant attributes were excluded as they can be parsed from
  attributes i.e. incident number, incident year and number of offenses
  can be derived from the service number field.
\item
  Irrelevant attributes such as address, apartment number etc. were
  excluded as they contributed little to our analysis.
\item
  Attributes with significant missing values were also omitted as they
  could not be used for our analysis i.e. Victim Package, Special Report
  (Pre-RMS), etc.
\end{itemize}

Let's review the datatypes of the selected attributes and the first 3
rows with headers

\subparagraph{Removing whitespace and enforcing a consistent naming
convention}\label{removing-whitespace-and-enforcing-a-consistent-naming-convention}

We have trimmed the leading/trailing spaces in the columns names that
we'd use for our analysis. We are also standardizing our data types by
eliminating whitespace in attribute names and replacing spaces in column
names with "underscores".

    \begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Most of the columns were observed to have the Object data type. These
  will be targeted for cleaning and/or standardized.
\end{itemize}

\subparagraph{Missing value analysis and data
cleansing}\label{missing-value-analysis-and-data-cleansing}

We did an analysis of the percentage of missing values in our dataset by
attribute to understand the extent of the issue with the subset we
selected. Given the large data set of 466K observations, we felt that
the impact of some missing values in certain attributes would not
significantly impact our analysis.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Explain missing data values

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Hate\_Crime}: There are only 376 values in this attribute.
    However, this is an important field that local jurisdictions must
    feed to the FBI as it is a key national metric. The default value is
    "blank" or "no" which explains why only those incidents with are
    investigated and deemed as true Hate Crimes had an explicit value of
    "YES".
  \item
    \textbf{Victim\_Age}: By design the ages of juveniles and minors
    have been willfully eliminated from the dataset due to sensitivity
    reasons
  \item
    \textbf{Victim\_Age\_at\_Offense}:Missing values are for the same
    reason as listed above
  \item
    \textbf{Victim\_Race}:Missing values were tagged as Unknown
  \item
    \textbf{Victim\_Gender}:Missing values were tagged as Unknown
  \item
    \textbf{Victim\_City}:Missing values in this attribute were assigned
    a value of "DALLAS" given the dataset is primarily focused on the
    Dallas area
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \subsubsection{Data Standardization}\label{data-standardization}

\paragraph{DIVISION}\label{division}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Standardized by making all values upper caps for improved grouping
\item
  Reasons for mistakes in data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Some mistakes in the data were clearly attributable to typos by
    either officers or dispatchers but were easily cleaned up such as
    sector, year, division etc.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \paragraph{COUNCIL DISTRICT}\label{council-district}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Standardized to address data errors where the leading "D" was missing
  so values are aligned to the correct division
\item
  Deletion and imputation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    We did not delete any data but excluded NULL values in some of the
    visualizations. Given most of the data in the set was categorical we
    chose not to impute data as we had a very large dataset of 466K
    observations.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

    \paragraph{VICTIM RACE (Ethnicity)}\label{victim-race-ethnicity}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Victim\_Race was aligned with the US Census Bureau standards on race
  and ethnicity as below. However, we did deviate from the US Census
  categories and retained Latino or Hispanic as a distinct ethnic group
  and did not merge it with American Indian or Alaska Native

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{White} -- A person having origins in any of the original
    peoples of Europe, the Middle East, or North Africa.
  \item
    \textbf{Black or African American} -- A person having origins in any
    of the Black racial groups of Africa.
  \item
    \textbf{American Indian or Alaska Native} -- A person having origins
    in any of the original peoples of North and South America (including
    Central America) and who maintains tribal affiliation or community
    attachment.
  \item
    \textbf{Asian} -- A person having origins in any of the original
    peoples of the Far East, Southeast Asia, or the Indian subcontinent
    including, for example, Cambodia, China, India, Japan, Korea,
    Malaysia, Pakistan, the Philippine Islands, Thailand, and Vietnam.
  \item
    \textbf{Native Hawaiian or Other Pacific Islander} -- A person
    having origins in any of the original peoples of Hawaii, Guam,
    Samoa, or other Pacific Islands.
  \item
    \textbf{Latino or Hispanic} -- A person from Mexico or South and
    Central America
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\emph{(Source: US Census Bureau)}

    \paragraph{VICTIM TYPE}\label{victim-type}

\begin{itemize}

\item
  Person values mapped to Individual

  Financial Institutions were mapped as Business

  Law Enforcement was mapped to Government
\item
  "na" and missing values replaced with Unknown

         \PY{n}{incident}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Drug\PYZus{}Related}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{incident}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Drug\PYZus{}Related}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{apply}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{get\PYZus{}binary\PYZus{}code}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}

    \begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The following attributes were either parsed or calculated from other
  attributes

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Number\_of\_Offense: Number of offenses per reported incident number
  \item
    Response\_Time: This is the time between receiving the call and
    dispatching it to officers for investigation
  \item
    Call\_Received: This is the time when the call was received by the
    911 call center.
  \item
    Call\_Cleared: This is the time when the call was cleared at the 911
    call center.
  \item
    Call\_Dispatch: This is the time when the call was dispatched by the
    911 call center to the police officer.
  \item
    Latitude: The latitude is parsed from the Location1 column.
  \item
    Longitude: The longitude is parsed from the Location1 column.
  \item
    Arrest\_Status: This is binary but converted to integers Yes=1, No=0
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Following attributes were Standardized to better visualize

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Hate\_Crime
  \item
    Gang\_Related\_Offense
  \item
    Drug\_Related\_Incident
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

    \subsubsection{Outlier Identification and
Cleanup}\label{outlier-identification-and-cleanup}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The Response time had outliers that needed to be addressed
\end{itemize}

            \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{outcolor}Out[{\color{outcolor}16}]:} count    468011.000000
         mean         36.923906
         std          76.342150
         min         -61.000000
         25\%           2.000000
         50\%           9.000000
         75\%          40.000000
         max       13290.000000
         Name: Response\_time, dtype: float64
\end{Verbatim}
        
    From the summary statistics results for the response attribute we
observe the following: - Average response time is approximately 37
minutes - It appears that the min is negative which does not make
practical sense - The max value also seems rather high at 13290 minutes
which equates to 221 hours or 9 days - Clearly these outliers need to be
addressed before progressing with further analysis

    \textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}
\# SIMPLE STATISTICS
\textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

    The exploratory statistics on the Response\_time calculated above proved
to be more insightful since time and date alone were not adequate to
answer the underlying questions of interest we had.

Per the Dallas Police Department, the trend of YTD average response
times for priority 1 calls has decreased year over year from 2017 to
2018 YTD from 8.28 minutes to 8.13 minutes respectively.

(Source:
https://www.dallaspolice.net/resources/CrimeReports/New\%20Response\%20Time\%20Report.pdf)

Missing and non-standard data values detected while calculating response
time appeared to be mainly due to recording errors. For the sake of this
analysis incidents having response times greater than 24 hours have been
ignored while performing simple statistical procedures.

The statistical summary of mean, std, min, max, IQR(25\%, 50\%, 75\%)
provided us with some more information on how to deal with these
situations. The histogram plotted enabled us to get better understanding
and visualization of the distributions.

We ran basic statistical summaries for non object or string datatypes.
Based on the descriptive statistical summaries below we can observe the
following.

    We didn't see much difference on the median of the response time in each
year. The Dallas city police department has maintained consistency on
the response times.

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{output_56_1.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    This violin plot indicates the response time distribution for the
THEFT/BMV (Burglary Motor Vehicle) offense. The violin plot shows the
relationship between the response time and the day of the week as well
as the arrest status.

It is evident that Mondays have a higher response time than other days
of the week. It also suggests that more arrests take place for this
specific offense type on Mondays than on any other day of the week.

    \textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}
\# VISUALIZE ATTRIBUTES
\textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

    To better understand and represent key findings we used graphical
components from pandas, matlab, PixieDust and even Power BI. This
enabled a much richer presentation of the facts and findings. We
selected the following attributes to visualize since they provided
greater insight into crime patterns by location and time, victim
profiles, arrests and the effect of response time.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Victim Type:} Victim type by incident count. This was best
  represented using a horizontal barchart.
\item
  \textbf{Arrest Status:} Arrests made against incidents reported and
  investigated. This was best represented using a horizontal barchart.
\item
  \textbf{Watch:} Categorizes which shifts or watch had the most
  reported incidents in the day.It is evident from the visual that Late
  night incidents are the highest followed by Evenings and Day.
\item
  \textbf{Calls Received by hour:} This is a distribution of incidents
  reported by hour of day and provides greater understanding of when the
  most incidents are received. It enables better planning of resources
  at the respective divisions.
\item
  \textbf{Division:} Incidents by Police Division gave insight into
  which areas in Dallas have the highest reported incidents
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}

  RED (0): No arrest made

  \textbf{Arrest Status:} Arrests made against incidents reported and
  investigated. This was best represented using a horizontal barchart.
\end{itemize}

It is evident from the above plot that while there were many reported
incidents, actual arrests occured in about 16\% of the reported
incidents.

    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{output_66_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \textbf{Watch Timings} - 1st watch = Late Night (12am-8am) - 2nd watch =
Days (8am-4pm) - 3rd watch = Evenings (4pm-12am) - Watch category 0 are
unknown and a result of missing data

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Watch:} Categorizes which shifts or watch had the most
  reported incidents in the day.It is evident from the visual that Late
  night incidents are the highest followed by Evenings and Day.
\end{itemize}

Above we see the trend of the \textbf{number of incidents reported by
the watch schedule} of the responding officer. Below, we look at another
view of this visual that shows us the \textbf{number of calls received
by hour}.

because this is the peak commute time.

    Next we look at the \textbf{distribution of UCR offences} for the top 10
categories. THEFT/BMV (Burglary Motor Vehicle) is the most commonly
reported incident followed by Mischief. The top 5 categories are
primarily around Theft/BMV, Mischief, Motor Vehicle, Residential
Burglary and Thefts.

    The above plot shows the overall trend of the number of dispatches. We
see a \textbf{significant spike} in the first half of 2018 which could
be either due to changes in their procedures for recording incidents or
other changes. We will explore this further in future analyses.

    The above plot shows that most of the reported offenses occur on
Highways, Streets and in Alleys followed by Single Family residences.

    \textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}
\# EXPLORE JOINT ATTRIBUTES
\textbf{-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-}

    Looking at response time across the years we see that response time
seems to be reducing. This is a good metric for the community as it
indicates the police are getting more responsive and also making more
arrests since quicker response times are related to more arrests. This
is a good trend for the Dallas police. We will analyze this in future
projects to classify these response times as part of our classification
analysis. We will conduct cross validations to further confirm our
hypothesis.

    Looks like Sector, Beat and reporting area is highly correlated with
X\_Coordinate, Y\_Coordinate, Arrest\_Status and Response Time. That
make sense as per the police department org chart setup as below:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Division: Dallas Partrol divisions including Centrol, North Central,
  Northeast, Northwest, South Central, Southeast, Southwest, and
  Headquarters.
\item
  Sector is an approach to policing whereby the service area of a police
  station is divided into smaller, manageable areas known as sectors.
  For each sector, the SAPS appoint a police official who is known as
  the sector commander.
\item
  Beat is the territory and time that a police officer patrols.Beat
  policing is based on traditional policing (late 19th century) and
  utilises the close relationship with the community members within the
  assigned beat to strengthen police effectiveness and encourage
  cooperative efforts to make a safer community. Beat police typically
  patrol on foot or bicycle which provides more interaction between
  police and community members.
\item
  Reporting Area: Each Beat contains neighborhoods which are referred to
  as Reporting Areas.
\item
  Here is the detail about how Dallas city police department is divided
  by council district, division, sector, beat and Reporting Area
  http://www.dallaspolice.net/abouts/maps
\end{itemize}

We also observe the following correlations: - Watch or shift is highly
correlated with Calls received by hour as we have observed previously in
our prior visualization.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Arrest status is also correlated to response time which we have
  highlighted above, but will further analyze below so the impact of
  early response time to arrests is confirmed.
\end{itemize}

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    Legend - GREEN (1): represents the arrest made during response to the
incident which are closely clustered with lower respose time. - BLUE
(0): represents arrests not made and aligns with higher response times

The columns with highest importance relate to response time.

The arrest is more likely when the response time is quicker, which is
supported from other visualizations as well.

    \textbf{PRELIMINARY OBSERVATIONS OF THE STATISTICS FROM THE ABOVE
GRAPH}: Arrests made is negatively correlated to response time, probably
because of the nature of the crime or incident. Higher the response time
indicates less of an emergency, but also indicates that no arrests were
made. This is in line with our previous observations where lower
response times resulted in more arrests. There is definitely a negative
correlation between higher response times and lower arrests.
\textbf{Negative correlation: -0.094}

Late night crimes are more prevalent and frequent as per data which is a
generally accepted norm as per social dynamics. Criminals clearly prefer
the late and early hours of the day for criminal activity probably
because since there are fewer people around and less daylight.

    \subsection{Explore Attributes and
Class}\label{explore-attributes-and-class}

Relationships Attributes we selected effect of Response time, arrests
made Drug related incidents Response time - will be classified into
categories such as good, bad and less than 30 minutes to help the police
dept. better manage resources in the process of dealing with incidents

Attributes like drug related incidents, gang incidents, hate crimes to
develop a scoring methodology for resource allocation by looking at
other variables and dynamics of arrests made

We will use random forests classifier algorithms to assist in our
analysis

  etc.

    profile.

    \subsection{Exceptional Work}\label{exceptional-work}

    To complement our Police Incident dataset we imported the following
datasets to provide greater insight for our analysis. This enabled us to
better understand arrest rates against incidents as well as consider
population factors that may play a critical role in effective policing
of a large metropolitan city like DALLAS.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Police Arrests dataset}
\item
  \textbf{Population Density dataset}
\end{itemize}

We also Geo-Mapped the data using Zip codes in Power BI to provide an
enhanced visualization of crime pockets in the Dallas area.

Our research found that there are two categories of offenses reported in
the NIBRS: Group A and Group B. It is important to determine which
category an offense belongs to because the reporting requirements for
Group A offenses and Group B offenses are different. Group A offenses
are used to report all incidents committed within a law enforcement
agency's jurisdiction to the NIBRS, whereas Group B offenses are used
only to report arrest data. The following criteria were used to
determine if a crime should be designated as a Group A offense: - The
seriousness or significance of the offense. - The frequency or volume of
its occurrence. - The prevalence of the offense nationwide. - The
probability of the offense being brought to law enforcement's attention.
- The likelihood that law enforcement is the best channel for collecting
data regarding the offense. - The burden placed on law enforcement to
collect data on the offense. - The national statistical validity and
usefulness of the collected data. - The FBI UCR Program's responsibility
to make crime data available not only to law enforcement but to others
having a legitimate interest in it.

There are 22 Group A offense categories making up 46 Group A offenses.
Each offense's corresponding UCR Offense Code precedes its name.
Immediately following the name of each offense name is an indication of
whether it involves a Crime Against Person, Crime Against Property, or
Crime Against Society.

There are 11 Group B offense categories. They encompass all of the
crimes that are not Group A offenses.

(Source:FBI.gov) )

    \begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Deletion and imputation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    We did not delete any data but excluded NULL values in some of the
    visualizations. Given most of the data in the set was categorical we
    chose not to impute data as we had a very large dataset of 466K
    observations.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}
